    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer9237599884115491147"><p>Richard Brautigan was a literary idol of the 1960s and 1970s whose comic genius and iconoclastic vision of American life caught the imagination of young people everywhere. He came of age during the Haight-Ashbury period and has been called the last of the Beats.” His early books became required reading for the hip generation, and on its publication Trout Fishing in Americ</p></span>
  <span id="freeText9237599884115491147" style="display:none"><p>Richard Brautigan was a literary idol of the 1960s and 1970s whose comic genius and iconoclastic vision of American life caught the imagination of young people everywhere. He came of age during the Haight-Ashbury period and has been called the last of the Beats.” His early books became required reading for the hip generation, and on its publication Trout Fishing in America became an international bestseller. An indescribable romp, the novel is best summed up in one word: mayonnaise.<br /> </p><p>This new edition includes an introduction by the poet Billy Collins, who first encountered Brautigan’s work as a student in California.</p></span>

<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">It is a good one, but Richard Brautigan was not a beat writer. He came into the scene on the tail end. He was associated with different Beat poets and<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">It is a good one, but Richard Brautigan was not a beat writer. He came into the scene on the tail end. He was associated with different Beat poets and writers but refused the title as a beat writer and the title of hippy writer.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer675430779" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13948296246994257967">I went up to Portland for the weekend to see my friend Trout Fishing in America get married. Portland is a great town and my friend is a great guy. Unfortunately I got the stomach flu or food poisoning or something and so I missed out on all but 45 minutes of his wedding, and on seeing old friends and all the drinking and the strip clubs and the late night Voodoo donuts and the arcade that everyone loves. All of that. So I just sat on the porch of the house we rented. It was a beautiful house an</span>
  <span id="freeText13948296246994257967" style="display:none">I went up to Portland for the weekend to see my friend Trout Fishing in America get married. Portland is a great town and my friend is a great guy. Unfortunately I got the stomach flu or food poisoning or something and so I missed out on all but 45 minutes of his wedding, and on seeing old friends and all the drinking and the strip clubs and the late night Voodoo donuts and the arcade that everyone loves. All of that. So I just sat on the porch of the house we rented. It was a beautiful house and the weather was beautiful too, perfect sitting on a porch weather. The house had a lot of books and I read 5 of them. 5! Still, I was sad to miss out on all the good times with my friends. Trout Fishing in America laughed and said you don’t much like to do those things anyways, you'd rather go it alone and read books so why are you complaining. I said that it feels better when you decline doing something instead of being forced not to do something. Trout Fishing in America said well isn’t that just the story of your life. Trout Fishing in America really gets me.<br /><br />Trout Fishing in America was written in 1967 and it is a unique kinda book. Hadrian describes it as <i>“a laughing walk through forests and as crooked as streams”</i> and I agree with him. It is random and loose and a collection of anecdotes and it doesn’t have a narrative per se but it does have a lot of goofy-smart moments and pleasurably odd bits of insight and fun, off-kilter imagery. Trout Fishing in America is a character in Trout Fishing in America. Richard Brautigan and Trout Fishing in America get along pretty well, I would say they were friends; it is the kind of friendship where Brautigan looks up to Trout Fishing in America, a symbolic kind of friend, one whose footsteps Brautigan tries to follow in. It is a sweet relationship and it is clear that Trout Fishing in America was a great influence on how Brautigan looks at the world. Trout Fishing in America is all over Trout Fishing in America, just everywhere.<br /><br />I’m writing this review and at the same time I’m writing a very technical response to some findings made by the local department of public health. I suppose that is my job, or at least a sizeable portion of it. I like writing, I love it actually, but does technical writing even count as writing? I like some flair in my prose or at least some elegance or some punch and when I put it in my technical writing it looks sorta funny. So I usually take it out again. I’d ask Trout Fishing in America what to do but I know what he’d say: what to do about what? It’s your life. Maybe go fishing. Or on a road trip. You like road trips, right? Trout Fishing in America is all about road trips and the things you see and find and learn about on road trips. Trout Fishing in America is about experiencing all of that and maybe forgetting about some of it too and how that doesn’t matter, life is funny that way, it’s less about what you remember and more about how those things affect you and shape how you look at the world and how you live in it. Your experiences make you <i>you</i>, they may not be deep experiences and you may not remember them all but you have them and that’s who you are and that’s how <i>you</i> are made. I would agree but I’m not sure I completely understand so I can’t explain why I agree. So I’ll just agree and leave it that.<br /><br />The book is seminal and I’m not sure why. It is definitely a pleasing experience and Brautigan has real flair with prose. Probably due to his many years as a poet before writing this book. I will remember this one but I’m not sure what I’m specifically taking away from it. I don’t think Trout Fishing in America will enjoy that comment so I am not going to mention it to him. Actually I don’t think Trout Fishing in America will care. It is what it is, he would probably say. But it is a lovely book. Kind of a hippy book, not a Protest The Man Let’s Start A Love Revolution kind of hippy, more of a Drop Out Of Society And Go On An Endless Road Trip Aren’t Folks Funny kind of hippy. I think that kind of hippy is a lovely kind of hippy. Trout Fishing in America is wry and deadpan and satirical and definitely strange but overall it is mainly a lovely kind of book.<br /><br />Trout Fishing in America made Richard Brautigan really popular, a cult figure and person who was admired by the counter-culture of the time. He became very popular very fast. He was an icon. When he was younger he was diagnosed with schizophrenia and depression, and even had to deal with electroshock therapy. In his career he made sure to switch it up a lot and wrote in different genres, in prose and in poetry. Despite his success and his fame, eventually his depression conquered him and he shot himself. It is a sad ending and I don’t want to think about it too much because it’s, well, depressing. I prefer to remember a phrase he once wrote: <i> "All of us have a place in history. Mine is clouds."</i> I think that is an enchanting phrase and Brautigan seems like an enchanting kind of guy.<br /><br />I remember I once went camping with Matt and Trout Fishing in America. It was a good trip and I got really close to those guys. We did a lot a lot of talking around the fire. Matt and Trout Fishing in America went fishing and caught some fish for us to eat. I’m not much of a fisherman so I mainly read in the boat. One time they went fishing right off of a shore and instead I waded in, got in there up to my neck. There were fish all around me and I remember feeling happy that they were safe around me and hoping that they wouldn’t bite me, that maybe they could sense I meant them no harm. The three of us did some hiking too. There was a huge tree that had fallen across a little creek, we crossed over it and just walked through the woods, no trail or anything. We came across a sort of mini waterfall and splashed around in it. I have a picture of Matt leaning into the waterfall, bracing himself with his arms against the rocks almost like he’s about to fuck the waterfall, and he has this funny expression on his face. I remember him saying something like this will be a good picture and I remember me and Trout Fishing in America laughing and saying something like well we all have different ideas about what makes a good picture. Overall I don’t remember a lot of the details but I remember the trip. We talked about it up in Portland. We couldn’t really recall many specifics but we felt it was definitely a good trip. Who cares about the specifics, it’s about what’s left in the mind, how we got to know each other, what we’re left with when the experience is over. I think that’s Trout Fishing in America’s perspective. I guess it’s mine too.<blockquote>
  <i>“He created his own Kool Aid reality and was able to illuminate himself by it.”</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="13948296246994257967" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText10100719548835588440" style="display:none">Trout Fishing in America, Richard Brautigan <br />Trout Fishing in America is a novella written by Richard Brautigan and published in 1967. It is technically Brautigan's first novel; he wrote it in 1961 before A Confederate General From Big Sur, which was published first. <br />The story takes place in 1957. A man named Lee Mellon believes he is a descendant of a Confederate general who was originally from Big Sur, California. This general is not in any books or records and there is no proof of his existence, although Mellon meets a drifter from the Pacific Northwest who has also heard of this general. Mellon seeks the truth of his own modern-day war against the status quo of the modern United States in light of the Confederacy's past struggle with the Union. The novel's overall theme is the struggle of the human mind's perceptions and beliefs versus reality.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: ماه دسمامبر سال 2005 میلادی<br />عنوان: صید قزل آلا در آمریکا؛ نویسنده: ریچارد براتیگان؛ مترجم: پیام یزدانجو؛ تهران، نشر چشمه، 1384؛ در 196 ص؛ چاپ دوم 1385؛ شابک 9643622150؛ چاپ سوم 1386؛ شابک: 9789643622152؛ چاپ پنجم 1389؛ چاپ ششم 1390؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان امریکایی - سده 20 م<br />عنوان: صید قزل آلا در آمریکا؛ نویسنده: ریچارد براتیگان؛ مترجم: هوشیار انصاری فر؛ تهران، نشر نی، 1385؛ در 207 ص؛ شابک: 9643138008؛ چاپ دوم 1385؛ چاپ سوم 1388؛<br /><br />رمان «صید قزل آلا در آمریکا‌‏» نوشته ی «ریچارد براتیگان»، با ترجمه ی جناب: «پیام یزدانجو» را نشر چشمه منتشر کرده است. براتیگان، از نسل نویسندگان دهه ی شصت سده ی بیستم میلادی هستند، و همین کتاب شاهكار ایشانست. آثار «براتیگان» بازتاب دهنده ی روح زمانه ی زندگی ایشان ست، زمانه ای که درک تازه از دنیای جدید داستانی را، تا همیشه مرهون نسل «براتیگان»ها خواهد ماند. ایشان را نویسنده ای نوگرا، و نامتعارف دانسته اند. با همین اثر بود که اشتهار یافتند. براتیگان در این اثر، به بهترین وجه از نبوغ خویش بهره گرفته، درهای دیگری را به روی ادبیات معاصر بگشوده است، رمان «صید قزل آلا در آمریكا»، نمونه ی ناب و منحصر به فردی، از ادبیات پسامدرن و زاییده ی ذهنی خلاق، خارق العاده و با تخیل تجربی، خیره كننده است. ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_2939527293" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2939527293" />
    <a title="Robin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36555254-robin"><img alt="Robin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1536187488p2/36555254.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2939527293?book_show_action=true">Jul 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Robin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Robin" href="/user/show/36555254-robin">Robin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780547255279" href="/book/show/7489048-trout-fishing-in-america">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Robin by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/73964263-fede">Fede</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=novella">novella</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/36555254-robin?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2939527293" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13023126028605533471">I was born well after the big splash of this little book that is synonymous with 1960s-1970s American counterculture. I read this well after dear Richard Brautigan took his own life. So you might say I missed the boat, the relevance of this strange little thing that I'll call a novel, but very loosely, given that it doesn't have a plot or narrative tension, or even characterization to speak of. <br/><br/>Maybe I did miss quite a bit, being from a different generation (and Canadian to boot), not being fami</span>
  <span id="freeText13023126028605533471" style="display:none">I was born well after the big splash of this little book that is synonymous with 1960s-1970s American counterculture. I read this well after dear Richard Brautigan took his own life. So you might say I missed the boat, the relevance of this strange little thing that I'll call a novel, but very loosely, given that it doesn't have a plot or narrative tension, or even characterization to speak of. <br /><br />Maybe I did miss quite a bit, being from a different generation (and Canadian to boot), not being familiar enough with the political significance of Benjamin Franklin or John Dillinger or mayonnaise. But I have to say that I was still completely delighted, lit up from the inside, in a way that only art can do.<br /><br />Reading Richard Brautigan makes the world sparkle for me, in a world that feels repetitive and limited, to say the least. He makes me (despite the fact that he is fishing for trout in a country that promises trout, to anyone who tries, and then doesn't find it, because he discovers that the creek isn't there and the waterfall is just a flight of wooden stairs) find humour and whimsy around me. It's all to do with his marvellous word choice, surprising little flowers popping up where you'd never expect them. A mother's voice is <i>filled with sand and string</i>, a man's eyes are <i>the shoelaces of a harpsichord</i>. A little boy is a "Kool-Aid wino", who at a tender age is like a cult leader performing a ritual, mixing the powder and water with reverence. A dog is so old he "hardly counted any more".<br /><br />As I mentioned, it's barely a novel. It's more poetry to me. It shouldn't surprise that Brautigan is also known for his poetry and has been called "the last of the Beats". The playful tenderness of his writing touched me, the originality inspired me, the dry, sarcastic wit impressed me. <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> is so a product of its time, but it what a mark it has left on the world, a lovely, heartfelt, nonconforming, tight-bellbottomed-jeans-wearing mark.<br /><br />Speaking of his poetry, I found this elsewhere - you're welcome:<br /><br /><b>Gee, You're So Beautiful That It's Starting To Rain</b><br /><br />Oh, Marcia,<br />I want your long blonde beauty<br />to be taught in high school,<br />so kids will learn that God<br />lives like music in the skin<br />and sounds like a sunshine harpsicord.<br />I want high school report cards<br />to look like this:<br /><br />Playing with Gentle Glass Things<br />A<br /><br />Computer Magic<br />A<br /><br />Writing Letters to Those You Love<br />A<br /><br />Finding out about Fish<br />A<br /><br />Marcia's Long Blonde Beauty<br />A+!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13023126028605533471" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1249838865" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1249838865" />
    <a title="Lyn" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn"><img alt="Lyn" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495541962p2/5253785.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1249838865?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn" href="/user/show/5253785-lyn">Lyn</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1249838865" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15713987859989824649">OK, well, first of all, it’s not about trout fishing in America.<br/><br/>Well, mostly not, sort of, well, see here’s the thing – <br/><br/>Richard Brautigan’s very unique 1964 publication blurs the line between prose and poetry, and in the same way that blue sounds a lot like jazz.<br/><br/>Yes, the similes.<br/><br/>Let’s visit some of Mr. Brautigan’s more bizarre and outlandish similes, and it is here that his readers first notice leaving a well-worn path.<br/><br/>“like a famous brain surgeon removing a disordered portion of the imagination</span>
  <span id="freeText15713987859989824649" style="display:none">OK, well, first of all, it’s not about trout fishing in America.<br><br>Well, mostly not, sort of, well, see here’s the thing – <br><br>Richard Brautigan’s very unique 1964 publication blurs the line between prose and poetry, and in the same way that blue sounds a lot like jazz.<br><br>Yes, the similes.<br><br>Let’s visit some of Mr. Brautigan’s more bizarre and outlandish similes, and it is here that his readers first notice leaving a well-worn path.<br><br>“like a famous brain surgeon removing a disordered portion of the imagination”<br><br>“the flesh about my body felt soft and relaxed like an experiment in functional background music”<br><br>“the fish taking all the bows like a young Jewish comedian talking about Adlai Stevenson”<br><br>And there are many, many like these that give the book it’s charming but absurd quality. The book is arranged into dozens of loosely connected vignettes and sketches, many with no apparent theme or notion of why they’re being told. <br><br>Also, the term “trout fishing in America” can be a person, as in “I went to visit my friend Trout Fishing in America” and it can be a place, a state of mind, and also, sometimes and obliquely, the literal act of trout fishing.<br><br>Funny? Yes, it can be funny, but for me the laughs came fewer and fewer as I turned the pages and became somewhat tiresome. This was not the Samuel Beckett or Bertold Brecht absurdism, or the drug induced gonzo weirdness of Hunter S. Thompson, but some form of sixties inventive and experimental lyric improvisation, like scat singing.<br><br>I recently visited Asheville, NC and had a great time, really enjoyed the natural setting, the accessible and ubiquitous beer and the art galleries. I observed in one gallery that although I liked just about everything I saw, I could not discern the distinction between paintings that would be worth $50,000 and another worth $500. Likewise, if I were to sample exquisite wine, I am sure that my palette is not discriminating enough to tell the difference between a very expensive Dom de la Romanee Conti Montrachet Grand Cru cote beaune le Puligny from Burgundy France and one of the fine table wines from Asheville. <br><br>I am, usually, able to keep my knuckles from dragging the floor when I walk. <br><br>But my point is that it is very likely that Brautigan’s work is masterful and inspiring and his great literary vision is just lost on me.<br><br>“He had a photograph taken of Existentialism and himself sitting at a sidewalk café.”<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1553798013i/27280514.jpg" width="236" height="312" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
  <a data-text-id="15713987859989824649" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1187903488" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1187903488" />
    <a title="s.penkevich" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6431467-s-penkevich"><img alt="s.penkevich" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1587089836p2/6431467.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1187903488?book_show_action=true">Feb 01, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="s.penkevich" class="user" itemprop="url" name="s.penkevich" href="/user/show/6431467-s-penkevich">s.penkevich</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Those in need of a smile</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to s.penkevich by: </span>
      Father John Misty
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=americana">americana</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6431467-s-penkevich?shelf=fishing">fishing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1187903488" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10089715433871298555">This book is an experience like few else. I could spend pages discussing this book but the following passage contains all the joy of the novel and is a well enough jumping-off point for the imagination and intellect to decipher the nature and importance of this novel that has been linked to the late-Beat generation¹<blockquote>
  <i>A little ways up from the shack was an outhouse with its door flung violently open. The inside of the outhouse was exposed like a human face and the outhouse seemed to say, 'The ol</i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText10089715433871298555" style="display:none">This book is an experience like few else. I could spend pages discussing this book but the following passage contains all the joy of the novel and is a well enough jumping-off point for the imagination and intellect to decipher the nature and importance of this novel that has been linked to the late-Beat generation¹<blockquote>
  <i>A little ways up from the shack was an outhouse with its door flung violently open. The inside of the outhouse was exposed like a human face and the outhouse seemed to say, 'The old guy who built me crapped in here 9,745 times and he's dead now and I don't want anyone else to touch me. He was a good guy. He bult me with loving care. Leave me alone. I'm a monument now to a good ass gone under. There's no mystery here. That's why the door's open. If you have to crap, go in the bushes like a deer."<br />"Fuck you," I said to the outhouse. "All I want is a ride down the river."</i>
</blockquote>I read this gem of a novel in its entirety on the 4th of July, 2013. Something about reading this on America's day of fireworks and independence while bathing in the glorious embrace of a local breweries 6-pack delight has some abstract importance to me that would be bastardized by my attempts to excavate it into concrete language for examination. All I know is that my sun-soaked soon-to-be-hung-over soul soared on a cloud of beauty that drank in each wonderful word from the pages. Particularly the chapter <i>The Hunchback Trout</i> where the narrow creek in which he is fishing is described '<i>like 12,845 telephone booths in a row with high Victorian ceilings and all the doors taken off and all the backs of the booths knocked out,</i>' transported me back to my teens when my dad would toss me into the car with his fishing gear and plant me into the Two Hearted River in Michigan's Upper Peninsula near where he was born and raised² Standing in the river with hand-me-down waiters that made me look like an overgrown and under-oranged oompa loompa, waiting for the appropriate time to go down stream and downwind so I could fail miserably at flyfishing while lighting up the secret rolled up treasures I'd brought for the weekend. I'm not much of a 'gamesman', but there was always something so empowering and surreal about being miles from civilization and feeling a part of the river and the trees that arched over my head (their spindly limbs being the landing place for many of my casts and causing me to spend half my time trying to get unstuck or untangled instead of actually fly-fishing). I would spend most of the day dreading actually catching a fish as it would mean having to touch it and unhook it and feel the shame and guilt and try not to show that I felt all weepy for hurting the fish in front of my father—a father who missed his calling by being placed into the early 2000's as a flesh-and-blood human instead of appearing as the t.v. father of a 50's sitcom that he really should have been (my friends used to refer to the Penkevich household as Mayberry)—as he beamed with pride and snapped a picture of me holding my catch. Somewhere exists one of these pictures snapped right at the moment that the current caught my legs out from under me and there I am disappearing into the stream with the fish held high above me. These moments are exactly what <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> means to me, and though this is not a novel I'd ever pass to my father, I like to associate the two in my mind and consider that as a sign of respect for a father that I truly appreciate and look up to even if we exist in such different worlds that bring about rifts between us. It is how to experience the idealized Americana moments with your father, even ones that never happened, as the novel reads much like a well-meaning father telling stories around a campfire at night with enough booze in his system to let loose all the sexual escapades and bawdy humor that is necessary for the story.<br /><br />Another episode of my not-worth-writing-about life that somehow fits with the novel and his accounts of tiny rebellions on society is when I had to write my submissions essay for Michigan State University. I had said to a friend 'they don't care what you write, it is <i>how</i> you write' and to prove my youthfully arrogant point I wrote my essay about fly-fishing with my father (the topic was how you view academics and how you have been a helpful part of your society). I got into MSU regardless. I feel Brautigan would have smiled at this tiny rebellion.<br /><br />Yet, somehow all this IS and isn't what this novel is. This novel is also a period of Americana that has been scrubbed away through time. It embraces that beatnik sensibility, the feeling that it is totally normal to stroll through town buried under the influence and ramble at everyone in a state of goodwill and not have the police immediately tossing you into the back of their car and bystanders pulling out their iphones to post the video of some raggedy beardo being manhandled by the boys in blue. This is a novel of good-natured fun that is sure to put a smile on the face of any who read it. I came across this novel after hearing it referenced in the song <a href="https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=36skxr7PAEU" rel="nofollow noopener">Tee-Pees 1-12</a> by Father John Misty, a song that also manages to capture the feeling of this novel. The take-away message here is that <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> is many things, and many things embrace the vibe of the novel while also not being an accurate way to describe it. If anything can be said about this book it is that <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> is all that is fun and good about living, like spending a day in the hot sun reading or drunk in a trout stream, or whatever it is that suits your fancy.<br /><b>4/5</b> <br /><br />¹ Did you really think I'd leave a review short and sweet? It's just not in my nature, and I apologize, but I also love to vomit my mind out into half-assed words. I feel labeling Brautigan as a 'late Beat' is an easy label, but one that doesn't serve him proper justice. Not like I have any better label for him, but it is akin to those who also consider Bukowski a Beat poet. The comparison makes sense, yet also misses the mark all at once. <br /><br />² Hemingway fanatics will recognize this river as the location and title of one of his most famous <a href="http://xroads.virginia.edu/~drbr/hem_river.html" rel="nofollow noopener">short stories</a> while beer fanatics will be salivating for the taste of that <a href="http://www.bellsbeer.com/brands/info/2" rel="nofollow noopener">delicious IPA by Bell's Brewery</a> that I am currently (and probably obviously) imbibing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10089715433871298555" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2683171824" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2683171824" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2683171824?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2683171824" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16376545828399639769">Published in 1967 but written in 1961 (and with a Best Before Date of June 1972), here we have a loopy, silly, zonked and floaty novel (if that’s what it is) that most surprisingly is actually in a bumbly zagzig manner all about trout fishing. Well, you know, kind of. The bizarro world version of trout fishing. This is so much like a rural version of Donald Barthelme (who was beginning to crank out his brilliant stories at exactly the same time) that I checked my Barthelme biography to remind my</span>
  <span id="freeText16376545828399639769" style="display:none">Published in 1967 but written in 1961 (and with a Best Before Date of June 1972), here we have a loopy, silly, zonked and floaty novel (if that’s what it is) that most surprisingly is actually in a bumbly zagzig manner all about trout fishing. Well, you know, kind of. The bizarro world version of trout fishing. This is so much like a rural version of Donald Barthelme (who was beginning to crank out his brilliant stories at exactly the same time) that I checked my Barthelme biography to remind myself what connections there were between these two American comedians. Only there was not one reference to Richard Brautigan. So then I saw that fans of RB bracket him with Kerouac and Burroughs, two other writers with whacked-out styles. So I checked my hefty biographies of those two gentlemen and what do you know. Not one mention of Richard Brautigan, not even a footnote. So now I’ve got the idea that this guy has been airbrushed from history. <br /><br />The humour is gentle. For instance, he finds a place called The Cleveland Wrecking Yard where there’s a used trout stream for sale. <br /><br /><i>"We’re selling it by the foot length. You can buy as little as you want or you can buy all we’ve got left. A man came in here this morning and bought 563 feet… We’re selling the waterfalls separately of course, and the trees and birds, flowers, grass and ferns we’re also selling extra. The insects we’re giving away free with a minimum purchase of ten feet of stream."</i><br /><br />And the humour is surreal too. <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> the novel (if that’s what it is) is a character in <i>Trout Fishing in America</i>. The way that works is… uh…. Well, you had to be there. It was kind of funny at the time. <br /><br />One thing I thought was funny but I don’t think Richard Brautigan intended it to be is how the narrator is traipsing all over various states to find a nice trout stream accompanied by his wife and daughter but the wife is never named and is always referred to as “my woman”. And the kid is called “the baby”. <br /><br />And finally, what many people remember about this tiny 120 page novel is the obsession RB has with outrageous similes. Almost everything comes with a simile. Here are a couple of typical ones:<br /><br /><i>I saw a woodstock. He had a long bill like putting a fire hydrant into a pencil sharpener, then pasting it onto a bird<br /><br />When the sun went behind a cloud, the smell of the sheep decreased, like standing on some old guy’s hearing aid, and when the sun came back again, the smell of sheep was loud, like a clap of thunder inside a cup of coffee.</i><br /><br />This novel would really get on your nerves if it was 300 pages long but it wasn’t and so it didn’t. A perfect gift to distract the old stoned hippy in your life from his Grateful Dead box sets for a couple of hours.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16376545828399639769" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_150453277" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150453277" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/150453277?book_show_action=true">Feb 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 0385288603" href="/book/show/4785153-trout-fishing-in-america">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2016">read-2016</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-5-stars">reviews-5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=brautigan">brautigan</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer150453277" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15305841205114389409"><b>Trout Fishing in Australia</b><br/><br/>A Richard Brautigan craze started in Australia when I was in secondary school. The focus was <i>"Trout Fishing in America",</i> even if I had already read and preferred <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150626677' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <i>"Confederate General from Big Sur".</i>
</a><br/><br/>I loved the zany, almost hallucinogenic brevity of his novels. They were as short and stimulating as a good high, and they were funny. They didn't necessarily have a plot, but they were full of acute, though relaxed, proto-stoner observations. Brautigan walked through life</span>
  <span id="freeText15305841205114389409" style="display:none"><b>Trout Fishing in Australia</b><br /><br />A Richard Brautigan craze started in Australia when I was in secondary school. The focus was <i>"Trout Fishing in America",</i> even if I had already read and preferred <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150626677" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <i>"Confederate General from Big Sur".</i>
</a><br /><br />I loved the zany, almost hallucinogenic brevity of his novels. They were as short and stimulating as a good high, and they were funny. They didn't necessarily have a plot, but they were full of acute, though relaxed, proto-stoner observations. Brautigan walked through life, detecting and reflecting on the comic side of absurdity, rather than the despair that was emphasised by the common or garden variety of Existentialism that prevailed in the media. Brautigan seemed to suggest that we should be glad just to exist, to be alive, stoned, immaculate. He looked into the abyss and laughed aloud. And it was infectious. Like nostalgia.<br /><br />After re-reading this novella, I was tempted to reduce my rating to four stars, but what the heck. That wouldn't be in the spirit of Brautigan, who was always generous with his sense of optimism and enjoyment.<br /><br />Below is a pastiche that will hopefully give you an idea of whether he is your kind of thing.<br /><br />P.S. It might help to know that he always wanted to end a novel with the word <i>"mayonaise".</i><br /><br /><b>What's So Funny about Trout Fishing in America?<br />[Assembled from Fragments of Richard Brautigan's Text]</b><br /><br /><i>"Trout Fishing in America".</i> What does it mean? Think hard about it for a minute. Why <i>"Trout Fishing in America"?</i> <br /><br />I thought about it for a while, hiding it from the rest of my mind. But I didn't ruin the day by secretly thinking too hard. It exists, like any other book, for no reason other than to amaze me.<br /><br />You might say, it won't do to write about <i>"Trout Fishing in America",</i> but then you might not say that at all. There is a romance about <i>"Trout Fishing in America".</i> Nobody else would have thought of it. Beautiful blondes followed me wherever I went. They were those great cuddly women of the past, wearing those pants they used to wear and those hightop, laced boots. They believed in their own immortality. Could be the fact that we were still alive had something to do with it. Hard to tell. We sat there and drank and talked about books. I can have a couple once in a while. I'm not supposed to, but it won't kill me. <br /><br />Trout fishing is one of the best things in the world for remembering what you read in books. It can also make you wonder whatever happened to the Zoot suit. I guess it was just a passing fad. Like World War II and the Andrews Sisters. During the war I saw a Deanna Durbin movie seven times in Great Falls, Montana. There was a darkness to that theatre different from any theatre I've been in since. <br /><br />Now I live in this place in San Francisco, this strange cabin above Mill Valley. We are a funny bunch, Pard and his girlfriend and Existentialism and me, all living here together. Pard met Existentialism in a sidewalk cafe in Paris, when he became an Existentialist and grew a beard. Now in his late thirties, Pard works at an avant-garde print shop that prints poetry and experimental prose. <br /><br />On the wall of the outhouse is a roll of toilet paper, so old it looks like a relative, perhaps a cousin, of the Magna Carta. I guess that's why Pard calls the outhouse the Magna Crappa.<br /><br />Are we agreed, ladies and gentlemen?<br /><br />"Agreed."<br /><br />What about the mayonaise?<br /><br />"What do you mean, what about the mayonaise?"</span>
  <a data-text-id="15305841205114389409" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer7788136872597519"><br/>Penned at the tail-end of the Beat movement, Brautigan's <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> is his surreal novel on the battle for humanity's soul waged between the high-stakes, ever expanding industrialism of the expiring 20th century and the salad days of nature worshiping 19th century. <br/><br/>The opening chapter of the book is fantastic and worth the investment in the novel just for those few pages alone. Even when the surrealism is thick Brautigan never lets go of his reader's hand. He wants us to see the pr</span>
  <span id="freeText7788136872597519" style="display:none"><br />Penned at the tail-end of the Beat movement, Brautigan's <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> is his surreal novel on the battle for humanity's soul waged between the high-stakes, ever expanding industrialism of the expiring 20th century and the salad days of nature worshiping 19th century. <br /><br />The opening chapter of the book is fantastic and worth the investment in the novel just for those few pages alone. Even when the surrealism is thick Brautigan never lets go of his reader's hand. He wants us to see the promise of potentiality in both his work and the world at large. He also writes some beautiful sentences, such as:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>The sheep lulled themselves into senseless sleep, one following another like the banners of a lost army.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />After finishing this book I looked to find out more of this author and learned that he killed himself in 1984 at the young age of 49. He suffered from clinical depression and paranoid schizophrenia. He once wrote, "All of us have a place in history. Mine is clouds."</span>

<div id="review_2932758281" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2932758281" />
    <a title="Fede" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73964263-fede"><img alt="Fede" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604333859p2/73964263.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2932758281?book_show_action=true">Aug 11, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fede" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fede" href="/user/show/73964263-fede">Fede</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=the-suicide-squad">the-suicide-squad</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=american-canadian">american-canadian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/73964263-fede?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2932758281" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11898844681034462118">Trout Fishing?<br/>In America? <br/><br/>Yes. Trout Fishing. <br/>In America. <br/><br/>Trout Fishing in America is the name we give to our quest for happiness. <br/>Trout Fishing in America is how we keep on looking for the time, the place, the lover, the friend, the chance we identify with happiness - OUR happiness - without even knowing. <br/>Journeying through life's elusive ghosts, edenic visions and sinister gloom, we're all in search of the perfect creek where the crystal clear water sounds like music and the rainbow trout spaw</span>
  <span id="freeText11898844681034462118" style="display:none">Trout Fishing?<br />In America? <br /><br />Yes. Trout Fishing. <br />In America. <br /><br />Trout Fishing in America is the name we give to our quest for happiness. <br />Trout Fishing in America is how we keep on looking for the time, the place, the lover, the friend, the chance we identify with happiness - OUR happiness - without even knowing. <br />Journeying through life's elusive ghosts, edenic visions and sinister gloom, we're all in search of the perfect creek where the crystal clear water sounds like music and the rainbow trout spawn eggs like promises of joy. <br />We're all Trout Fishing in America, each of us carrying around his fishing tackle, throwing his rod in some rushing stream or quiet pond along the way - hoping it's the good one, hoping a few nice trout will make our day... if only for today.<br />Because more often than not Trout Fishing in America is the empty creel, the snapped rod, the slippery rock that makes one fall into the river; it's the bad weather that spoils a lovely afternoon by the lake; it's the moment <br /><br />"(...) I ended up being my own trout and eating the slice of bread myself." <br /><br />Trout Fishing in America is indeed one of the most delicate allegories in modern literature, a pastiche made of 1967 hipster vibes and the author's peculiar interiorisation of feelings and experiences, so beware: if a linear plot is what you're after, this book is definitely not your thing. <br />Because, you see, this is not a novel. TFiA is a collection of short narrative vignettes, actually prose-poems revolving around memories, impressions and digressions - a memoir of the soul. But if you're willing to look beyond the confined spaces of genres and definitions, oh, you're going to be rewarded. These pages are painfully beautiful, melancholically playful and incredibly deep in their deceiving naivety; in fact there's something odd about these almost surreal interiors and landscapes, or rather glimpses of a reality beneath the surface of our familiar geography:<br /><br />"We were leaving in the afternoon for Lake Josephus, located at the edge of the Idaho Wilderness, and he was leaving for America, often only a place in the mind." <br /><br />Between Pop-culture and zen tableau: Brautigan's America is the one he was familiar with. The land of Campbell's soup and Kool-Aid, of winos and housewives, bums and writers, pimps and whores, cheap hotels and middle-class campings; the place where all was happening, right there and then, where the Love generation was dreaming to change the world while the Napalm lords were thriving in its incineration.<br />TFiA is therefore a nostalgic dreamscape of lost innocence and an artistically clever analysis of the late 60s status quo, although Brautigan's imagery is too poetic to fit any truly political content - and that's what makes this short book such a delightful reading experience. <br />It takes a pure soul to write so beautifully of trivial things, and this is definitely the case. <br /><br />This was my first Brautigan: no doubt an amazingly good start. I actually own a 1989 collection of three works by him (<a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12568.Trout_Fishing_in_America___The_Pill_vs__the_Springhill_Mine_Disaster___In_Watermelon_Sugar" title="Trout Fishing in America / The Pill vs. the Springhill Mine Disaster / In Watermelon Sugar by Richard Brautigan" rel="nofollow noopener">Trout Fishing in America / The Pill vs. the Springhill Mine Disaster / In Watermelon Sugar</a>) but I thought each work deserved to be reviewed as a stand alone, as I expect the other books to be little gems just like this one. <br /><br />On with his poetry now!</span>
  <a data-text-id="11898844681034462118" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_451064668" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/451064668" />
    <a title="David Schaafsma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma"><img alt="David Schaafsma" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574997804p2/269235.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/451064668?book_show_action=true">Aug 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Schaafsma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Schaafsma" href="/user/show/269235-david-schaafsma">David Schaafsma</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/269235-david-schaafsma?shelf=fiction-20th-century">fiction-20th-century</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer451064668" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8055471044002033834">“I always wanted to write a book that ended with the word Mayonnaise.” <br/><br/>“Truth is stranger than fishin’.” <br/><br/>Richard Brautigan published Trout Fishing in America in 1967. In a way it reminds me of a goofier, lighter, more absurd version of Kerouac’s On the Road, with lots of traveling around in nature. I also see connections to Kerouac in that both struggled with depression; Kerouac documented his decline through alcoholism in Big Sur, and Brautigan killed himself after writing So the Wind Won’t Blo</span>
  <span id="freeText8055471044002033834" style="display:none">“I always wanted to write a book that ended with the word Mayonnaise.” <br /><br />“Truth is stranger than fishin’.” <br /><br />Richard Brautigan published Trout Fishing in America in 1967. In a way it reminds me of a goofier, lighter, more absurd version of Kerouac’s On the Road, with lots of traveling around in nature. I also see connections to Kerouac in that both struggled with depression; Kerouac documented his decline through alcoholism in Big Sur, and Brautigan killed himself after writing So the Wind Won’t Blow it all Away. Brautigan was sometimes referred to as the hippie Hemingway because he wrote well, wrote here as Hemingway did often of trout fishing, and, well, you know how Hemingway ended up. Hemingway was more sophisticated (and the better writer, with serious aspirations, obviously) an aficionado of exotic drinks; Brautigan was more of a wine and pot guy, though he did write a lot. Interesting that all three of these writers seemed to embrace life with such gusto and humor and then, had those hard endings.<br /><br />But this book is not Brautigan’s end! It’s like Hem’s In Our Time or On the Road; it’s a kind of ecstatic beginning; Brautigan typed much of the first draft of Trout (which sounds like it might be a serious treatise on fishing by Izaak Walton but is so not) on a long weekend camping with his wife and baby daughter, who are (seemingly) referenced throughout the book, and most of it happens outside, in the pacific northwest. He does fish for trout throughout the book, but the “plot” is mainly a meandering trip through Brautigan’s mind. And it is fun and funny and is not quite like anything before it. Vonnegut is a better and more serious (and better) writer. Tom Robbins (Even Cowgirls Get the Blues) is also goofy and wryly observant. But Brautigan’s writing and comic insights are really fine, seemly infused by some of the purest drugs to be found growing in his own backyard. <br /><br />“I remember mistaking an old woman for a trout stream in Vermont, and I had to beg her pardon. <br />‘Excuse me,’ I said. ‘I thought you were a trout stream.’ <br />‘I'm not,’ she said.”<br /><br />“Used trout stream for sale. Must be seen to be appreciated.”<br />“How are you selling it?”<br />“By the foot. A guy came by this afternoon and bought 556 feet of it. The waterfall is extra, of course. Insects are free with any purchase.” <br /><br />Brautigan is not exactly anti-literary but he likes to make fun of the world of literary fiction, of literature:<br /><br />“After he graduated from college, he went to Paris and became an Existentialist. He had a photograph taken of Existentialism and himself sitting at a sidewalk cafe. He was wearing a beard and he looked as if he had a huge soul, with barely enough room in his body to contain it.” He begins with a quotation from Benjamin Franklin, he makes references throughout to Great Literature, to Art, he makes footnotes, though the information doesn’t necessarily lead anywhere. There is the literary repetition of images like trout fishing and a Ben Franklin statue and mayonnaise jars. <br /> <br />Sometimes he writes a little like Hemingway:<br /><br />“The girl was very pretty and her body was like a clear mountain river of skin and muscle flowing over rocks of bone and hidden nerves.” <br /><br />Well, okay, that would be similar to Hemingway’s use of the conjunction; after that, he’s mainly in an altered state, but a happy one, as also here: <br /><br />“One spring afternoon as a child in the strange town of Portland, I walked down to a different street corner, and saw a row of old houses, huddled together like seals on a rock.” <br /><br />In this altered state he thinks Trout Fishing of America is a trout stream, or a man named Trout Fishing in America Shorty, or a hotel, or whatever comes up in his imagination.<br /><br />This is a funny book that made me happy for a few hours to be alive. I liked visiting it like an old friend, one who apparently drinks gallons of Gallo Port by the jug and remains pretty clever. I had been looking for an antidote of sorts to the sad madness of Kerouac’s Big Sur and the sad madness (but now anger) of Roth’s Everyman (though I think those are great books, don’t get me wrong; they just weighed on me a bit) and I found it in Brautigan. Hey, man, are you done rollin’ that? Pass it here!<br /><br />PS: In March 1994, a teenager named Peter Eastman Jr. from Carpinteria, California legally changed his name to "Trout Fishing in America". He now teaches English in Japan. At around the same time, National Public Radio reported on a young couple who had named their baby "Trout Fishing in America".</span>
  <a data-text-id="8055471044002033834" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_267650178" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/267650178" />
    <a title="Paul" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4643325-paul"><img alt="Paul" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535048395p2/4643325.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/267650178?book_show_action=true">Jan 25, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul" href="/user/show/4643325-paul">Paul</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4643325-paul?shelf=american-novels">american-novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer267650178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13218635038874691553">Short and completely off the wall; published in 1967 and immediately a success with the counterculture. The favourite book of a number of ageing hippies I have known! <br/>It has been compared to Kerouac and Burroughs, but I think that is mistaken; it is a different type of approach to the world. The chapters are short and informal. Trout Fishing in America appears as a person/persons throughout and has spawned at least one modern band and several sets of parents naming their unfortunate offspring Tr</span>
  <span id="freeText13218635038874691553" style="display:none">Short and completely off the wall; published in 1967 and immediately a success with the counterculture. The favourite book of a number of ageing hippies I have known! <br />It has been compared to Kerouac and Burroughs, but I think that is mistaken; it is a different type of approach to the world. The chapters are short and informal. Trout Fishing in America appears as a person/persons throughout and has spawned at least one modern band and several sets of parents naming their unfortunate offspring Trout Fishing in America. Regarded as a 60s classic, it was actually written in 1961 and I think it has more of a 50s feel to it. Brautigan wrote much of it on a camping holiday with his wife and daughter. That for me is the key, some of the flights of imagination (going to the garden centre to pick up trout streams by the foot with waterfalls, flowers, insects as extras) and the odd names feel like something that a parent might make up for a child; almost a bed time story. Some of the more adult parts seem like later add ons and parts are certainly Brautigan's reflections on life in his America. <br />On the whole it is entertaining and odd, but I think it is as much a story for a child as anything else.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13218635038874691553" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2879829289" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2879829289" />
    <a title="Antonomasia" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6249204-antonomasia"><img alt="Antonomasia" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605185148p2/6249204.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2879829289?book_show_action=true">Jul 03, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antonomasia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antonomasia" href="/user/show/6249204-antonomasia">Antonomasia</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/23482165-trout-fishing-in-america">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=american">american</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=novella">novella</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=humour">humour</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=outdoors-and-nature">outdoors-and-nature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=divine-comedy-booklovers">divine-comedy-booklovers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=decade-1960s">decade-1960s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6249204-antonomasia?shelf=environment">environment</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2879829289" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer980685344024099307">____ is done with Trout Fishing in America.<br/>I am 45% done with Trout Fishing in America.<br/>____ made progress with Trout Fishing in America. <br/>The title phrase of <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> is repurposed in this surreal, freewheeling novella to mean many different things. These sentences, auto-generated by Goodreads status updates, would not seem out of place in it, and could refer to an activity, a discrete inanimate object, a person or an abstraction. <br/><br/><i>Trout Fishing in America</i> is very now, and also ver</span>
  <span id="freeText980685344024099307" style="display:none">____ is done with Trout Fishing in America.<br />I am 45% done with Trout Fishing in America.<br />____ made progress with Trout Fishing in America. <br />The title phrase of <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> is repurposed in this surreal, freewheeling novella to mean many different things. These sentences, auto-generated by Goodreads status updates, would not seem out of place in it, and could refer to an activity, a discrete inanimate object, a person or an abstraction. <br /><br /><i>Trout Fishing in America</i> is very now, and also very 1960s, and this coexistence of contradictions is absolutely typical of the book. <br /><br />It is a novella made up of fragments and vignettes, many of these apparently autobiographical - such a 2010s format - and at times a male narrator is involved in babycare without thinking it remarkable, in a way that has probably only started to seem normal over the last 10 years. <br /><br />In the afterword to this edition, (originally an introduction, now replaced by an endearing one by Neil Gaiman that has fewer spoilers), American poet <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/438.Billy_Collins" rel="nofollow noopener">Billy Collins</a> wrote in 2010 <i>”How Brautigan might strike young readers of today is hard to calculate.”</i> In 2019 one can more easily assume: that they would criticise the Indian-erasure in the narrative, as it gives the impression there was nothing in the American wilderness before white men; that they would point out the reductive sketches of some PoC and the epithets that have now become dysphemisms; that it would seem too male-centred (even if he did apparently do his bit with childcare, and include a conversation about contraception that's more matter of fact than anything you might think would be in Anglo-American literature written in 1961). And for some, the scenes where fish are killed will only add to these problems.<br /><br />(In <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> we also find out what 1960s young people thought of bits of the past. I love this sort of thing. <i>“A lot of cars, airplanes and vacuum cleaners and refrigerators and things that come from the 1920s look as if they had come from the 1890s. It’s the beauty of our speed that has done it to them, causing them to age prematurely into the clothes and thoughts of people from another century.”</i> and <i>"It was the classic forest lookout photograph I have seen before, from that America that existed during the 1920s and 30s.... People in those days liked to take that photograph and they liked to be in it."</i>)<br /><br />Collins also highlights the repeated collisions of the “natural” with the “mechanical”, and if <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> is typical of Brautigan, his work must be fertile ground for ecocriticism. And as nature and environmental damage looks like the next big theme in literary fiction, this is another way in which the book feels remarkably contemporary. <br /><br />As Collins says: <i>This essential American tension between the wilderness and the city climaxes in the long chapter “The Cleveland Wrecking Yard” where the narrator goes to buy a used trout stream, which is being sold “by the foot length,” only to discover that waterfalls, birds, plants, insects, and animals are also on sale. The radical vision of a consumer-driven world is made more absurd by the folksy, deadpan exchanges between narrator and salesman</i>. This is near the end of the book, developing what’s previously seen in Brautigan’s other industrialisations of nature such as: <i>Always questing for a good trout stream, the narrator walks up into a canyon “as if I were entering a department store.” “I caught three trout in the lost and found department,” he adds, like a Henny Youngman one-liner. Trout are likened to “holes punched in a card that had just come out of a computer,”</i> I realised during ‘The Cleveland Wrecking Yard that all this is a goddamn sight more honest than most of the New Nature Writing about how contemporary Global North people actually interact with nature, and about how nature really is now, and has been shaped in the Anthropocene, with a <a href="https://www.theguardian.com/environment/2018/oct/30/humanity-wiped-out-animals-since-1970-major-report-finds" rel="nofollow noopener">60% decline in wildlife</a> since the time <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> was published. (The New Nature Writing is partly about preserving and treasuring what is still there, but it could also be seen as inhabiting the denial phase of mourning the loss.)<br /><br /><i>Trout Fishing in America</i> doesn’t only make weird metaphors about the commodification of nature; it also evokes the American wilderness and made me understand why and how people love it. (I’ve rarely grokked that, in a way that probably won’t make sense, especially to Americans. I think I first saw much of the American outdoors in in the staid series <i>Gentle Ben</i>, which was repeated a lot on 1980s UK TV. I generally felt interactions with it were kind of fake, probably based on a combination of old British snobbery about the “shallowness” of American history, and thinking about relatives who’d come back to England from imperial white settler colonies.) There is a sense of enchantment about the American wilderness in this book, and that it’s a place and an idea you could, and might want to get lost in - something I last felt from a few scenes in <i>Twin Peaks</i>. (Brautigan is also from the Pacific Northwest.) <br /><br />But all that sounds very serious for what is also an incredibly silly book, full of absurd humour that you will either get or you won’t. (If you don’t, you will probably cringe and call it ‘zany’, or say it ‘misses the mark’.) For me, most of the time, it was that delightful sort of thing where you find laughter bubbling up before you could ever explain why. It’s just <i>funny</i>; surreal scenarios and mad metaphors. (I love novel metaphors anyway.)<br /><i>“a gigantic hound-like flashlight”</i>, or <i>“a few stubborn rainbow trout, seldom heard from, but there all the same, like certified public accountants”</i>. <br />There is that specificity of detail that makes things funny in context: a camper <i>“eating a dehydrated beef stroganoff dinner”</i>, or, about a different minor character in another story, <i>“Then he bought a mule, George, from Pleasanton.”</i><br /><br />Sometimes it's just for the sheer fizziness of the connections sprouting, rather than humour itself:<br /><i>The dandelions were suddenly more sheep than flower, each petal reflecting wool and the sound of a bell ringing off the yellow. But the thing that smelled the most like sheep, was the very sun itself. When the sun went behind a cloud, the smell of the sheep decreased, like standing on some old guy’s hearing aid, and when the sun came back again, the smell of the sheep was loud, like a clap of thunder inside a cup of coffee.</i> [complete with a clause that wouldn't pass muster now, but is easily revisable to something okay]<br /><br />There is, in one chapter, a comically long list of fishing books. I suspect they are fictional, and I decided to have a pre-internet reading experience by not looking them up to check, and letting them sit in that space of uncertainty such things always used to.<br /><br />The socioeconomics of the characters are unlike most other novels. Brautigan or his narrator sometimes hangs out with winos like they were any other mates and there isn’t a sense of being of a different class from them. (Though there is a good case for saying the specific wino named Trout Fishing in America Shorty is othered.) A place is <i>“nothing fancy, not like where the swells go”</i>. Swells are other people. The narrator/s isn’t/aren’t materialistic. Though he does, in at least one vignette, have a car. (To what extent did that mean being well off? Most Americans need them just to get to work.) He seems so uncommodified and unselfconscious compared with almost any published writer now, and I feel like there is a level of authenticity here that’s hard to achieve if someone is thinking much about authenticity. (ETA: This is a slightly edited version of what I wrote before reading Brautigan's <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Brautigan#Background" rel="nofollow noopener">Wikipedia biography</a>. He was from a poor working-class family, with the added instability of having a succession of stepfathers while he was growing up. The reason he never reads like a middle-class writer playing class tourist is because that's absolutely not what he was.)<br /><br />Though there is this friend who may give the impression that nothing much ever changes in certain corners of the arts: <i>“Now in his late thirties Pard works at a print shop for $1.35 an hour. It is an avant-garde print shop. They print poetry and experimental prose. They pay him $1.35 an hour for operating a linotype machine. A $1.35 linotype operator is hard to find, outside of Hong Kong or Albania.”</i> But there isn’t the same sense of precarity and doom that suffuses the work of shoestring young writers now. (Or that there was in the 19th century.) Probably because 1960s people weren’t defeated by housing costs and housing access, and it was easier to pick up random work if you needed to, and it felt like a lot of things were getting better.<br /><br />I’m not sure I’ve ever read anything that is so postmodern and so natural at the same time. The more I write about the book, the more impressed with it I am. And in the last quarter I wanted to give it 5 stars. Yes, there are a few misfires, e.g. the bit about the lumberjack and the ‘squaw’. But otherwise so much came together, and it was funny, and surprisingly complex if you wanted it to be, whilst also being easy to read and very relaxing. The previous day, when I’d planned to finish the book, I’d been thrown by a bafflingly ill-natured online discussion and was not feeling very keen on posting about books or perhaps even reading them. But there was so little left the next day I may as well try and finish it - and the sentences in <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> had the right simplicity, and often content, that it became possible with not to much effort to focus on them in meditative fashion, and it was wonderfully relaxing. It turned out to be exactly the right book at the time. It felt as if it wouldn’t have worked so well had I tried to read something with more convoluted sentences, or more traditional rules and structures, or less silliness, or less outdoors, or less… hippieness? <br /><br />It probably is like fishing, eccentric like Walton and doing its own thing in laid-back fashion. Reminded me of some primary school worksheet question that asked, some black and white illustrations that would probably look incredibly 70s/80s now: “which activity would you most like to do with an adult?”, and I was the only one who circled fishing. Not because of actively wanting to kill fish, nor did I even like eating most fish, but I’d picked up the idea somewhere that it would be nice to just sit there quietly, unbothered by people, and look at the pond or river and feel the weather change over hours, waiting for an occasional nonverbal signal to action. <br /><br />Richard Brautigan was the author I thought I was least likely to read from The Divine Comedy's <a href="https://www.azlyrics.com/lyrics/divinecomedy/thebooklovers.html" rel="nofollow noopener">The Booklovers</a> (other than Samuel Richardson, whom I figured didn't even count, as hardly anyone reads his giganovels in full unless they are on an Eng Lit degree course). I rarely heard about Brautigan anywhere else, so he didn't seem *important* like other classic authors, and the blurb on a copy of his <i>Sombrero Fallout</i> had looked, in my teens, like my idea of boring. But most of Brautigan's most famous books are really short... so when I decided to attack this list again, he suddenly became an obvious choice. And <i>On Watermelon Sugar</i> didn't really appeal, despite the prescience of his calling anything iDeath, because I don't like authoritarian dystopias. Of his popular books, it was <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> I most liked the sound of. I liked it very much, and I doubt I ever would have read it if it weren’t for the song, so, not for the first time, thank you very much Neil Hannon!<br /><br />[4.5]<br />(Read &amp; reviewed July 2019.)</span>
  <a data-text-id="980685344024099307" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
