<div id="review_2339493241" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2339493241" />
    <a title="Ian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/31384949-ian"><img alt="Ian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1584889764p2/31384949.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2339493241?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian" href="/user/show/31384949-ian">Ian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781782113805" href="/book/show/23400286-trout-fishing-in-america">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=4-star-lit-fiction">4-star-lit-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=usa">usa</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=modern-classics">modern-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/31384949-ian?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2339493241" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8998153468731985902">A re-read for me. I first read this as a teenager in the 1970s. I remember that when any of my schoolfriends saw the book’s cover, they would ask “Why on Earth are you reading a book about trout fishing in America?” I would tell them it wasn’t really about that and of course they would ask “so what’s it about then?” and I wasn’t able to tell them. <br/><br/>I still can’t.<br/><br/>The best I can say is that the book is a series of snippets from Brautigan’s life. Some of these are taken from his childhood; some are </span>
  <span id="freeText8998153468731985902" style="display:none">A re-read for me. I first read this as a teenager in the 1970s. I remember that when any of my schoolfriends saw the book’s cover, they would ask “Why on Earth are you reading a book about trout fishing in America?” I would tell them it wasn’t really about that and of course they would ask “so what’s it about then?” and I wasn’t able to tell them. <br /><br />I still can’t.<br /><br />The best I can say is that the book is a series of snippets from Brautigan’s life. Some of these are taken from his childhood; some are from a road trip he took with an unnamed female companion (usually referred to by the phrase “my woman”) and their infant daughter; and some from his life in the San Francisco area. Parts of it, especially the chapter entitled “The Cleveland Wrecking Yard” are a satire on the commercial exploitation of rural America, and quite a good one at that. Throughout the book, Brautigan simply lets his imagination roam free.<br /><br />The novel was written in 1961 but wasn’t published until 1967, which of course was the perfect year to publish something as off-centre as this. As the 70s progressed Brautigan gradually fell out of step with the Zeitgeist and by the time the 80s arrived nobody was reading his books.<br /><br />I probably enjoyed this more when I first read it 40-odd years ago. I’m a bit too old for it now. Still, it’s a short and easy read, it’s quite funny in parts, and it deserves 4 stars for its sheer craziness.<br /><br />Far out, man.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8998153468731985902" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_33793182" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33793182" />
    <a title="Erik Graff" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff"><img alt="Erik Graff" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1299888977p2/974210.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/33793182?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erik Graff" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erik Graff" href="/user/show/974210-erik-graff">Erik Graff</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 0385288603" href="/book/show/4785153-trout-fishing-in-america">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Americans</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Erik by: </span>
      Tom Kosinski
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/974210-erik-graff?shelf=literature">literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer33793182" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5437738284879417821">Although my life was not very pleasant from the time of moving to Park Ridge in fifth grade until the beginning of high school, things began to pick up by the sophomore year. I joined the Social Science Society at school,a club dominated by older students who were predominately bookish and left-leaning. I made my first real friends, Rich Hyde and Hank Kupjack, both of whom also belonged to Tri-S.<br/><br/>Things got even better by the junior and senior years. It was the end of the sixties and what had hap</span>
  <span id="freeText5437738284879417821" style="display:none">Although my life was not very pleasant from the time of moving to Park Ridge in fifth grade until the beginning of high school, things began to pick up by the sophomore year. I joined the Social Science Society at school,a club dominated by older students who were predominately bookish and left-leaning. I made my first real friends, Rich Hyde and Hank Kupjack, both of whom also belonged to Tri-S.<br /><br />Things got even better by the junior and senior years. It was the end of the sixties and what had happened on the coasts years before was beginning to penetrate the Chicago suburbs. We were a minority to be sure, but Maine South with over four thousand students, a minority can be pretty large.<br /><br />The suburban counterculture was seen as comprising three overlapping groups. All thought of themselves as different and most felt some kinship, if not identification, with the others. The largest group, numerically speaking, were those who took illegal drugs and weren't ashamed of the practice. Indeed, some were quite knowledgeable, quite serious. Virtually no one took dangerous or addictive drugs. Pot and the hallucinagons were the substances of choice. Although there were a few drinkers, we probably drank a lot less than average students or the generations before or after ours. Drink was not serious. A second group may have been peculiar to our town. It was the earliest that had a self-professed identity. They called themselves, among other things, "the Meek" and had a particular interest in the arts. Naturally, the arts they publicly identified with were not those normally judged to be fine arts, but rather performance works which they did themselves, shows that they put on themselves, books by authors out of the mainstream, experimental stuff, cutting-edge stuff. I made a lot of friends in that group, a couple of them being the first self-professed homosexuals I'd ever met. The third group were the politicals. Although most of us, for this was my primary self-definition, were at least "informed by Marxism", and almost all were students very interested in and sympathetic to what the general society would have called "leftist", a couple of "us" were actually libertarians of one sort or another. The decisive thing, other than personality, was being radical, questioning the status quo.<br /><br />Now-a-days, and even then if one relied on 'Life Magazine' for information, people think the sixties counterculture was physically identifiable by hairstyle and clothing. This was certainly not the case with us. I probably got my first bellbottoms after Dad did in the seventies. Likewise, few guys had long hair until it became fashionable. If anything, we just worried less about how we looked and resented the tyranny of fashion (or, to we in the third group, "commodity fetishism").<br /><br />So, who did we read back then? The books that circulated widely among my friends, the books I read because my friends recommended them, were by such as Herman Hesse, Kurt Vonnegut, Leonard Cohen, Peter Ginsberg, Hunter Thompson, Lawrence Ferlinghetti, Timothy Leary, Fyodor Dostoevsky, Albert Camus, Paramahansa Yogananda, Mohandas Gandhi, Friedrich Nietzsche, John Lily and, of course, Richard Brautigan.<br /><br />Tom Kosinski was the one who recommended Brautigan as I recall, but if it hadn't been him it would have been another, so popular was the author in our circles. And why? Well, generally he's very, very funny. He even looked funny, especially considering that he was a grown man. He smoked pot. He celebrated the odd ball, the eccentric, the ones rejected by or rejecting of normative culture. I loved him and read one of his books after another.<br /><br />At his best, still now, the Kosinski reminds me of him.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5437738284879417821" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3042472440?book_show_action=true">Nov 12, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Foust" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Foust" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust">Lee Foust</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3042472440" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11836903121499189328">Although I preferred <i>So the Wind Won't Blow It All Away</i> and even <i>The Abortion</i>, I guess, because they paid a certain homage to standard narrative forms in novels while also thrilling us with Brautigan's inimitable cleverness and quirky style of narration, <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> is also amazing, but more so, for me, because of how much more experimental and fun it is than the other novels of his that I've read. There's only the vaguest sense of a narrative here, even perhaps only a few scenes fr</span>
  <span id="freeText11836903121499189328" style="display:none">Although I preferred <i>So the Wind Won't Blow It All Away</i> and even <i>The Abortion</i>, I guess, because they paid a certain homage to standard narrative forms in novels while also thrilling us with Brautigan's inimitable cleverness and quirky style of narration, <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> is also amazing, but more so, for me, because of how much more experimental and fun it is than the other novels of his that I've read. There's only the vaguest sense of a narrative here, even perhaps only a few scenes from a vacation strung together between other short chapters on life in various locations--San Francisco's North Beach neighborhood prime among them, but also some childhood memories and a summer spent in Mill Valley--and treating of various topics, always framed as if they, too, were about trout fishing even when they aren't by simply replacing a key word or a title with the novel's title. This was, as I said, very clever, entertaining, experimental, and showed off Brautigan's wonderful talent for short, pithy, and wise vignettes. And is also, in a way, postmodern. Much in the way that Vonnegut's intrusive narrators and occasional meta-narrative are postmodern. Aplomb is I guess the operative narrative device. Telling it like it is, but Brautigan spices this up with frequently absurd similes that always make me smile. This one is pretty lighthearted (unlike <i>So the Wind Won't Blow It All Away</i> and bits of <i>The Abortion</i>).<br /><br /><br />(Aside) Here's a list of my favorite novels set in San Francisco:<br /><br />1. <i>The Royal Family</i> by William Vollman<br />2. <i>The Subterraneans</i> by Jack Kerouac<br />3. <i>Pick-up</i> by Charles Willeford<br />4. <i>The Maltese Falcon</i> by Dashiell Hammet<br />5. <i>Trout Fishing in America</i> by Richard Brautigan</span>
  <a data-text-id="11836903121499189328" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/174120178?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="M. Sarki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="M. Sarki" href="/user/show/5562041-m-sarki">M. Sarki</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer174120178" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8601835493801102455">Though I do believe this book was extremely important in its time I am not convinced it is any longer. I did enjoy rereading it as it brought back old and pleasant memories of a time first-called The Generation of Love. Richard Brautigan, after years of writing poetry and learning how to write a good sentence, made this first stab at composing a version of what he would come to call his very first novel. This first work made the rounds of many publishers and was pretty much shelved for other tit</span>
  <span id="freeText8601835493801102455" style="display:none">Though I do believe this book was extremely important in its time I am not convinced it is any longer. I did enjoy rereading it as it brought back old and pleasant memories of a time first-called The Generation of Love. Richard Brautigan, after years of writing poetry and learning how to write a good sentence, made this first stab at composing a version of what he would come to call his very first novel. This first work made the rounds of many publishers and was pretty much shelved for other titles of Brautigan such as his second novel <i>A Confederate General from Big Sur</i> that was loosely based on a friend of his and was more "plot driven" than <i>Trout Fishing in America</i>. Of course, we all know that this, his actual first novel, is what made him famous and led to his enormous fame and fortune that could not last nor endure his depressive state that was never far from present throughout his entire life of forty-nine years. A clever first book, often brilliant in spots, with sounds resembling the best lyrics of a young Bob Dylan who was also in process of finding his own voice in that same time along the by-ways of America. </span>
  <a data-text-id="8601835493801102455" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer430742129" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14571331289834737894">Finished this a few weeks ago, and would have much sooner, but I actually stopped reading it for a week 10 pages from the end, because I really wanted it to linger, though the good news is that it's short enough that I will probably reread it sooner/more frequently than some of my other favorites.<br/><br/>The kind of book that I loved without entirely knowing why, but a few highlights:<br/>--Brautigan is from Tacoma, Washington, and I am from Washington, and started this on my way back from my vacation there,</span>
  <span id="freeText14571331289834737894" style="display:none">Finished this a few weeks ago, and would have much sooner, but I actually stopped reading it for a week 10 pages from the end, because I really wanted it to linger, though the good news is that it's short enough that I will probably reread it sooner/more frequently than some of my other favorites.<br /><br />The kind of book that I loved without entirely knowing why, but a few highlights:<br />--Brautigan is from Tacoma, Washington, and I am from Washington, and started this on my way back from my vacation there, so I was somewhat predisposed to want to like it.<br />--The setting is the PNW and Northern California, which is my favorite region of the country, so again, predisposed...<br />--Brautigan has a sort of dry, matter-of-fact sense of humor, dovetailed with a sort of absurdity, in a way that reminded me of British humor, except a down-and-out, drunk and poor version, and way less uptight.<br />--Also reminded me a bit of Bukowski, but I don't really know why, because Brautigan is much more subtle/introverted, and subtlety was not Bukowski's thing, so maybe just the boozy, California aspect.<br />--Basically a touch of all sorts of things I like in literature; compelling characters, a good sense of humor, a touch of the absurd/random, the occasional dash of magical realism, a setting near and dear to my heart (the West), and that something you just can't put your finger on.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/12933866-edward?shelf=2016">2016</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1463139521" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14372659738505342376">I'm not really sure what this short book was about, except that I can say for certain that in some way it was about Trout Fishing in America. But then what is Trout Fishing in America? Is it a person, a pastime, a place, a proxy for life, the universe and everything? Maybe. Or maybe it's just some dude talking shit. Either way, it's whimsical, amusing, poetic shit, and like actual shit, it is probably best taken for what it is and not examined too closely.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13623062925240988759" style="display:none">Okay, I’ll admit it: I don’t get it.<br /><br />I may need to start singing the “Small World” song, however, as I happened to notice that this book is dedicated to someone named <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/207820.Ron_Loewinsohn" title="Ron Loewinsohn" rel="nofollow noopener">Ron Loewinsohn</a>. “Huh,” I thought. “I had a professor at Berkeley named Ron Loewinsohn. I wonder if it could be the same guy?”<br /><br />Two minutes of Wikipedia research reveals: yup! Same dude! Apparently he and Brautigan were good friends back in the day. Many years later, Loewinsohn is vying for the title of my favorite college professor. (It’s between him and an enthusiastic Irishman who wore waistcoats all the time and liked to talk about his <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1265.Jane_Austen" title="Jane Austen" rel="nofollow noopener">Jane Austen</a> fanfiction. That Loewinsohn’s neck-and-neck with this guy shows what an awesome lecturer he was.) Now I’m tempted to write him an email. Maybe something like, “Hi, not sure you remember me—I wrote that essay on <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2956.The_Adventures_of_Huckleberry_Finn" title="The Adventures of Huckleberry Finn by Mark Twain" rel="nofollow noopener">Huckleberry Finn</a> you really liked? Just wanted to say thanks for being such a great teacher. P.S. PLEASE EXPLAIN RICHARD BRAUTIGAN AND TROUT FISHING IN AMERICA TO ME. I DON’T GET IT.”<br /><br />I’m sure that would go over well.</span>

  <span id="freeText7110988621410488577" style="display:none">This book is one of the most overrated pieces of shit in the history of publishing. After listening to every hippie I had ever come into contact with praise it fulsomely, I had the misfortune to finally read it for myself when I was about 19. Like many teenagers, I used to smoke a lot of what was colloquially known, once upon a time, as reefer. Even that, however, wasn't enough to make this book even remotely enjoyable. I recently got a copy for free, and read it again just to double-check my earlier opinion. It was not a pleasant experience. Don't believe what you hear- the only brilliant thing about this book is the fact that Brautigan pulled such an incredibly successful scam in actually getting paid for writing it.</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer165389921" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1727185322742805377">Richard Brautigan is the favorite author of a woman I loved and finally after years I have read him. I appreciate his short story 1/3 1/3 1/3 and his poem Machines of Loving Grace. I honor him for enduring for years the deep sorrow that eventually caused him to take his own life. But regretfully I ask: what the hell is this? Local color? An attempt at humor ("you had to be a plumber to fish that creek")? Is it supposed to be friendly and accessible like Rod McKuen("next year somebody else will h</span>
  <span id="freeText1727185322742805377" style="display:none">Richard Brautigan is the favorite author of a woman I loved and finally after years I have read him. I appreciate his short story 1/3 1/3 1/3 and his poem Machines of Loving Grace. I honor him for enduring for years the deep sorrow that eventually caused him to take his own life. But regretfully I ask: what the hell is this? Local color? An attempt at humor ("you had to be a plumber to fish that creek")? Is it supposed to be friendly and accessible like Rod McKuen("next year somebody else will have to go trout fishing")? Ever listen to an NPR program and wonder why it exists? That's my question after flipping through "Trout Fishing in America." At one point Brautigan writes that he "always wanted to write a book that ended with the word Mayonnaise." Sure enough, his closing line is: "Sorry I forgot to give you the mayonaise." So at least he sort of realized that dream, since most people won't notice the missing N. Perhaps literary critics will amass countless pages exploring the letter's absence. I found it to be like a night watching TV. There's nothing on, the experience feels aimless and pointless yet somewhat pleasurable. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1727185322742805377" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_4146761" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4146761" />
    <a title="Dan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/253465-dan"><img alt="Dan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1186332341p2/253465.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4146761?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dan" href="/user/show/253465-dan">Dan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4146761" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11109579627612366530">Richard Brautigan's easy-going, beyond informal style should be the easiest thing in the world for today's internet-saavy to read - the freestyle association, onomatopoeia, and occasional anthropomorphism fits right in with any lolcats-humorist. In his most famous work, Brautigan fires off tiny snippets of genius with Trout Fishing in America inserted as whatever concept or person he focuses on. Occasionally, he ties some of the concepts together on a whim. Amidst all this very 60's attitude and</span>
  <span id="freeText11109579627612366530" style="display:none">Richard Brautigan's easy-going, beyond informal style should be the easiest thing in the world for today's internet-saavy to read - the freestyle association, onomatopoeia, and occasional anthropomorphism fits right in with any lolcats-humorist. In his most famous work, Brautigan fires off tiny snippets of genius with Trout Fishing in America inserted as whatever concept or person he focuses on. Occasionally, he ties some of the concepts together on a whim. Amidst all this very 60's attitude and writing style, Brautigan finds time to settle into scenes like "Worsewick, a beautiful vignette of hideously real sexuality and descriptive language that puts emphasis on the least notable of details, all to add up to the most comprehensive picture possible because of it. Elsewhere in "Hotel Trout Fishing In America" he crams a whole <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/80176.Winesburg__Ohio" title="Winesburg, Ohio by Sherwood Anderson" rel="nofollow noopener"> Winesburg, Ohio</a> into one crappy hotel, and a significantly lower number of pages. Whether its fishing, winos, recipes, sex, terrorists, or a doe with a head full of strawberries, Brautigan's voice sweeps it all up with you included.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11109579627612366530" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <span id="freeText8361805452966757234" style="display:none">This is the first time I've met Trout Fishing in America. And although I fished almost everyday in my youth and caught hundreds of Trout, I never realized that the guy with me was Trout Fishing in America. We'd always stop at Ledet's Supermarket and buy bread, ham, and a small jar of mayonnaise on our way to the trout rooms. We'd sit in our small boat with corks bobbing in the room and eat ham sandwiches. We'd look at the sky and see rabbits, angels, or toaster ovens in the clouds. And we'd appreciate the freedom to sit in a little boat with corks bobbing and eating ham sandwiches... with mayonnaise.<br /><br />This book is a travel book of sorts. It reintroduced me to America. And streams. With trout. In another time. Trout Fishing in America is alright.<br /><br /><i>I remember mistaking and old woman for a trout stream in Vermont, and I had to beg her pardon.<br /><br />'Excuse me,' I said. 'I thought you were a trout stream.'<br /><br />'I'm not,' she said.</i></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer75012918548453901">richard brautigan is probably my favorite person in the world. which i suppose is kind of sad, because he's not in it anymore. this book is great, and very, very funny. his metaphors and one-liners are i think at an all-time high here. i don't like it as much as <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/302666.The_Hawkline_Monster_A_Gothic_Western' title='The Hawkline Monster A Gothic Western by Richard Brautigan' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Hawkline Monster: A Gothic Western</a> because it's still pretty fragmented, and not as much as the stories in <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/115207.Revenge_of_the_Lawn_Stories_1962_1970___Rebel_Inc__S__' title='Revenge of the Lawn Stories 1962-1970 ("Rebel Inc" S.) by Richard Brautigan' rel='nofollow noopener'>Revenge of the Lawn: Stories 1962-1970</a> because the chapters aren't emotionally affecting for the most part. the book is primar</span>
  <span id="freeText75012918548453901" style="display:none">richard brautigan is probably my favorite person in the world. which i suppose is kind of sad, because he's not in it anymore. this book is great, and very, very funny. his metaphors and one-liners are i think at an all-time high here. i don't like it as much as <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/302666.The_Hawkline_Monster_A_Gothic_Western" title="The Hawkline Monster A Gothic Western by Richard Brautigan" rel="nofollow noopener">The Hawkline Monster: A Gothic Western</a> because it's still pretty fragmented, and not as much as the stories in <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/115207.Revenge_of_the_Lawn_Stories_1962_1970___Rebel_Inc__S__" title="Revenge of the Lawn Stories 1962-1970 (&quot;Rebel Inc&quot; S.) by Richard Brautigan" rel="nofollow noopener">Revenge of the Lawn: Stories 1962-1970</a> because the chapters aren't emotionally affecting for the most part. the book is primarily comedic. which is nice. it's nice to think of brautigan happy. it kinda bums me out that i prefer the books he wrote when he was bummed out. except, of course, for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/88013.Willard_and_His_Bowling_Trophies" title="Willard and His Bowling Trophies by Richard Brautigan" rel="nofollow noopener">Willard and His Bowling Trophies</a>, which pretty much just makes me want to die and i wish he'd never written it and never been sad and hopeless enough to write it. but anyway. this is a good book, and funny. very, very funny.<br /><br /><i>After he graduated from college, he went to Paris and became an Existentialist. He had a photograph taken of Existentialism and himself sitting at a sidewalk cafe. Pard was wearing a beard and he looked as if he had a huge soul, with barely enough room in his body to contain it.</i></span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked

    }
  }

In this distres<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText4785959060753863206\" style=\"display:none\">In Guignol\'s Band, first published in France in 1943, Céline explores the horror of a disordered world. The hero, the semi-autobiographical Ferdinand, moves through the nightmare of London\'s underworld during the years of World War I. In this distressing setting, he meets pimps and prostitutes, pawnbrokers and magicians, policemen and arsonists. He sees social and physiological decomposition as these processes unfold along parallel lines of development. The illusions of existence are nakedly exposed. The narrative erupts in Céline\'s characteristic elliptical style. His splintered sentences and scatology reflect his fury at the fragmentation of experience and at his own impotence in the face of it. Out of his rage, he forces the meaninglessness back on itself, and the exuberance of his struggle triumphs in the comic exaggeration of satire. Ultimately, his subject is not death but life, and he responds to it by a strengthened commitment to the sensual and concrete. His hallucinatory world is so vividly realized that it does, indeed, challenge the reality of the reader\'s more conventional world.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"4785959060753863206\" href=\"#\" Burroughs wrote Junky, his first novel. It is a candid eye-witness account of times and places that are now long gone, an unvarnished field report from the American post-war underground. Unafraid to portray himself in 1953 as a confirmed member of two socially-despised under classes (a narcotics addict and a homosexual), Burroughs was writing as a trained anthropologist when he unapologetically described a way of life - in New York, New Orleans, and Mexico City - that by the 1940\'s was already demonized by the artificial anti-drug hysteria of an opportunistic bureaucracy and a cynical, prostrate media. For this fiftieth-anniversary edition, eminent Burroughs scholar Oliver Harris has painstakingly recreated the author\'s original text, word by word, from archival typescripts and places the book\'s contents against a lively historical background in a comprehensive introduction. Here as well, for the first time, are Burroughs\' own unpublished introduction and an entire omitted chapter, along with many &quot;lost&quot; passages, as well as auxiliary texts by Allen Ginsberg For more than fifty years Gaddis collected notes for a book about the mechanization of the arts, told by way of a social history of the player piano in America. In the years before his death in 1998, he distilled the whole mass into a fiction, a dramatic monologue by an elderly man with a terminal illness. Continuing Gaddis\'s career-long reflection on those aspects of corporate technological culture that are uniquely destructive of the arts, Agape Agape is a stunning achievement from one of the <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer18299548651398228111"><b>Richard Brautigan</b> was an American novelist, poet, and short-story writer. Born in Tacoma, Washington, he moved to San Francisco in the 1950s and began publishing poetry in 1957. He started writing novels in 1961 and is probably best known for his early work <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/63913.Trout_Fishing_in_America' title='Trout Fishing in America' rel='nofollow noopener'>Trout Fishing in America</a>. He died of a self-inflicted gunshot wound in 1984.</span>
  <span id="freeText18299548651398228111" style="display:none"><b>Richard Brautigan</b> was an American novelist, poet, and short-story writer. Born in Tacoma, Washington, he moved to San Francisco in the 1950s and began publishing poetry in 1957. He started writing novels in 1961 and is probably best known for his early work <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/63913.Trout_Fishing_in_America" title="Trout Fishing in America" rel="nofollow noopener">Trout Fishing in America</a>. He died of a self-inflicted gunshot wound in 1984.</span>
